<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:D%C3%A9but_de_pas_de_danse_du_Zangb%C3%A9to_-_B%C3%A9nin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1836" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/D%C3%A9but_de_pas_de_danse_du_Zangb%C3%A9to_-_B%C3%A9nin.jpg/220px-D%C3%A9but_de_pas_de_danse_du_Zangb%C3%A9to_-_B%C3%A9nin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/D%C3%A9but_de_pas_de_danse_du_Zangb%C3%A9to_-_B%C3%A9nin.jpg/330px-D%C3%A9but_de_pas_de_danse_du_Zangb%C3%A9to_-_B%C3%A9nin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/D%C3%A9but_de_pas_de_danse_du_Zangb%C3%A9to_-_B%C3%A9nin.jpg/440px-D%C3%A9but_de_pas_de_danse_du_Zangb%C3%A9to_-_B%C3%A9nin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:D%C3%A9but_de_pas_de_danse_du_Zangb%C3%A9to_-_B%C3%A9nin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Local African ceremony in Benin featuring a <a href="/wiki/Zangbeto" title="Zangbeto">zangbeto</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The traditional beliefs and practices of African people are highly diverse beliefs that include various <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_religion" title="Ethnic religion">ethnic religions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mol_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-mol-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Generally, these traditions are <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">scriptural</a> and passed down from one generation to another through folk tales, songs, and festivals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> include belief in an amount of higher and lower gods, sometimes including a supreme creator or force, belief in spirits, <a href="/wiki/Veneration_of_the_dead" title="Veneration of the dead">veneration of the dead</a>, use of <a href="/wiki/Magic_(supernatural)" title="Magic (supernatural)">magic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traditional_African_medicine" title="Traditional African medicine">traditional African medicine</a>. Most religions can be described as <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> with various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheistic" title="Pantheistic">pantheistic</a> aspects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mol_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-mol-1">[1]</a></sup> The role of humanity is generally seen as one of harmonizing nature with the supernatural.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mol_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-mol-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup>
</p>
<div aria-labelledby="mw-toc-heading" class="toc" id="toc" role="navigation"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2 id="mw-toc-heading">Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spread">Spread</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Spread">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Igbo_medicine_man.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="364" data-file-width="416" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Igbo_medicine_man.jpg/220px-Igbo_medicine_man.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Igbo_medicine_man.jpg/330px-Igbo_medicine_man.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/72/Igbo_medicine_man.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Igbo_medicine_man.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early-20th-century <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo</a> medicine man in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, West Africa</div></div></div>
<p>Adherents of traditional religions in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> are distributed among 43 countries and are estimated to number over 100 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclbrit_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Encyclbrit-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lugira_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lugira-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although most Africans today are adherents of <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> or <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, African people often combine the practice of their traditional beliefs with the practice of Abrahamic religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-googlemix_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-googlemix-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The two Abrahamic religions are widespread across Africa, though mostly concentrated in different areas. They have replaced indigenous African religions but are often adapted to African cultural contexts and belief systems. Abrahamic religious beliefs, especially monotheistic elements, such as the belief in a single creator god, was introduced into traditionally polytheistic African religions rather early.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>Followers of traditional African religions are also found around the world. In recent times, religions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_religion" title="Yoruba religion">Yoruba</a> religion, are on the rise. The religion of the Yoruba is finding roots in the Islands of the Caribbean and portions of Central and South America. In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_African_Vodun" title="West African Vodun">Voodoo</a> is more predominant in the states along the Gulf of Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Basics">Basics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Basics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">Animism</a> builds the core concept of traditional African religions. This includes the worship of <a href="/wiki/Tutelary_deity" title="Tutelary deity">tutelary deities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nature_worship" title="Nature worship">nature worship</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">ancestor worship</a> and the belief in an <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>. While some religions adopted a <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">pantheistic</a> worldview, most follow a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a> system with various gods, spirits and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernatural_being" title="Supernatural being">supernatural beings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> Traditional African religions also have elements of <a href="/wiki/Fetishism" title="Fetishism">fetishism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Veneration_of_relics" title="Veneration of relics">veneration of relics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ganvie_Voodoo_Dancer_(21596115932).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ganvie_Voodoo_Dancer_%2821596115932%29.jpg/220px-Ganvie_Voodoo_Dancer_%2821596115932%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ganvie_Voodoo_Dancer_%2821596115932%29.jpg/330px-Ganvie_Voodoo_Dancer_%2821596115932%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ganvie_Voodoo_Dancer_%2821596115932%29.jpg/440px-Ganvie_Voodoo_Dancer_%2821596115932%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ganvie_Voodoo_Dancer_(21596115932).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vodun" title="Vodun">Vodun</a> dancer enchanting gods and spirits, in <a href="/wiki/Ganvie" title="Ganvie">Ganvie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benin" title="Benin">Benin</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Traditional African, like most other ancient traditional religions around the world, were based on <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral traditions</a>. These traditions are not religious principles, but a cultural identity that is passed on through stories, myths and tales, from one generation to the next. The community and ones family, but also the environment, plays an important role in one's personal life. Followers believe in the guidance of their ancestors spirits. Among many traditional African religions, there are spiritual leaders and kinds of priests. These persons are essential in the spiritual and religious survival of the community. There are mystics that are responsible for healing and 'divining' - a kind of fortune telling and counseling, similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamans" title="Shamans">shamans</a>. These traditional healers have to be called by ancestors or gods. They undergo strict training and learn many necessary skills, including how to use natural herbs for healing and other, more mystical skills, like the finding of a hidden object without knowing where it is. Traditional African religion believe that ancestors maintain a spiritual connection with their living relatives. Most ancestral spirits are generally good and kind. Negative actions taken by ancestral spirits is to cause minor illnesses to warn people that they have gotten onto the wrong path.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Native African religions are centered on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">ancestor worship</a>, the belief in a <a href="/wiki/Spirit_world_(Spiritualism)" title="Spirit world (Spiritualism)">spirit world</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernatural_being" title="Supernatural being">supernatural beings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> (unlike the later developed concept of <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">faith</a>). Deceased humans (and animals or important objects) still exist in the spirit world and can influence or interact with the physical world. Forms of <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">polytheism</a> was widespreaded in most of ancient African and other regions of the world, before the introduction of Islam, Christianity, and <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>. An exception was the short-lived monotheistic religion created by Pharaoh <a href="/wiki/Akhenaten" title="Akhenaten">Akhenaten</a>, who made it mandatory to pray to his personal god Aton (see <a href="/wiki/Atenism" title="Atenism">Atenism</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> This remarkable change to traditional Egyptian religion was however reverted by the next Pharaoh of Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Okwu_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Okwu-25">[25]</a></sup> High gods, along with other more specialized deities, ancestor spirits, territorial spirits, and beings, are a common theme among traditional African religions, highlighting the complex and advanced culture of ancient Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Okwu_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Okwu-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
Some research suggests that certain monotheistic concepts, such as the belief in a high god or force (next to other many other gods, deities and spirits, sometimes seen as intermediaries between humans and the creator) were present within Africa, before the introduction of Abrahamic religions. These indigenous concepts were different from the monotheism found in Abrahamic religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convehret_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-convehret-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Okwu_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-Okwu-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Koku_Dancer.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Koku_Dancer.jpg/220px-Koku_Dancer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Koku_Dancer.jpg/330px-Koku_Dancer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Koku_Dancer.jpg/440px-Koku_Dancer.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Koku_Dancer.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traditional Koku dancer, bearing strong similarities to religious dancers of the <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_folk_religion" title="Dravidian folk religion">Dravidian folk religion</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_India" title="Southern India">Southern India</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Traditional_African_medicine" title="Traditional African medicine">Traditional African medicine</a> is also directly linked to traditional African religions. According to Clemmont E. Vontress, the various religious traditions of Africa are united by a basic <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">Animism</a>. According to him, the belief in spirits and ancestors is the most important element of African religions. Gods were either self-created or evolved from spirits or ancestors which got worshiped by the people. He also notes that most modern African folk religions were strongly influenced by non-African religions, mostly Christianity and Islam and thus may differ from the ancient forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional African religions generally hold the beliefs of life after death (a spirit world or realms, in which spirits, but also gods reside), with some also having a concept of reincarnation, in which deceased humans may reincarnate into their family lineage (blood lineage), if they want to, or have something to do.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Americans" title="Nigerian Americans">Nigerian American</a> professor of indigenous African religions at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jacob_Olupona" title="Jacob Olupona">Jacob Olupona</a> summarized the many traditional African religions as complex <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a> religious traditions and beliefs of the African people before the Christian and Islamic "colonization" of Africa. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">Ancestor veneration</a> has always played a "significant" part in the traditional African cultures and may be considered as central to the African worldview. Ancestors (ancestral ghosts/spirits) are an integral part of reality. The ancestors are generally believed to reside in an ancestral realm (spiritworld), while some believe that the ancestors became equal in power to deities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The defining line between deities and ancestors is often contested, but overall, ancestors are believed to occupy a higher level of existence than living human beings and are believed to be able to bestow either blessings or illness upon their living descendants.
Ancestors can offer advice and bestow good fortune and honor to their living descendants, but they can also make demands, such as insisting that their shrines be properly maintained and propitiated. A belief in ancestors also testifies to the inclusive nature of traditional African spirituality by positing that deceased progenitors still play a role in the lives of their living descendants.</p></blockquote>
<p>Olupona rejects the western/Islamic definition of <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">Monotheism</a> and says that such concepts could not reflect the complex African traditions and are too simplistic. While some traditions have a supreme being (next to other deities), others have not. Monotheism does not reflect the multiplicity of ways that the traditional African spirituality has conceived of deities, gods, and spirit beings. He summarizes that traditional African religions are not only religions, but a worldview, a way of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceremonies">Ceremonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ceremonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>West and Central African religious practices generally manifest themselves in communal ceremonies or divinatory rites in which members of the community, overcome by <i>force</i> (or <i>ashe</i>, <i>nyama</i>, etc.), are excited to the point of going into meditative trance in response to rhythmic or driving drumming or singing. One religious ceremony practiced in <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Okuyi" title="Okuyi">Okuyi</a>, practiced by several <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">Bantu</a> ethnic groups. In this state, depending upon the region, drumming or instrumental rhythms played by respected musicians (each of which is unique to a given deity or ancestor), participants embody a deity or ancestor, energy or state of mind by performing distinct ritual movements or dances which further enhance their elevated consciousness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Karade,_B._pages_39-46_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Karade,_B._pages_39-46-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p><p>When this trance-like state is witnessed and understood, adherents are privy to a way of contemplating the pure or symbolic embodiment of a particular mindset or frame of reference. This builds skills at separating the feelings elicited by this mindset from their situational manifestations in daily life. Such separation and subsequent contemplation of the nature and sources of pure energy or feelings serves to help participants manage and accept them when they arise in mundane contexts. This facilitates better control and transformation of these energies into positive, culturally appropriate behavior, thought, and speech.  Also, this practice can give rise to those in these trances uttering words which, when interpreted by a culturally educated initiate or diviner, can provide insight into appropriate directions which the community (or individual) might take in accomplishing its goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Spirits">Spirits</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Spirits">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_African_mythological_figures" title="List of African mythological figures">List of African mythological figures</a></div>
<p>Followers of traditional African religions pray to various spirits as well as to their ancestors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> This includes also nature, elementary and animal spirits. The difference between powerful spirits and gods is often minimal. Most African societies believe in several “high gods” and a large amount of lower gods and spirits. There are also some religions with a single supreme being (<a href="/wiki/Chukwu" title="Chukwu">Chukwu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nyame" title="Nyame">Nyame</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olodumare" title="Olodumare">Olodumare</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ngai" title="Ngai">Ngai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roog" title="Roog">Roog</a>, etc.).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Some recognize a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dualistic_cosmology" title="Dualistic cosmology">dual</a> god and goddess such as <a href="/wiki/Mawu" title="Mawu">Mawu-Lisa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>Traditional African religions generally believe in an <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>, one or more <a href="/wiki/Spirit_world_(Spiritualism)" title="Spirit world (Spiritualism)">Spirit worlds</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">Ancestor worship</a> is an important basic concept in mostly all African religions. Some African religions adopted different views through the influence of Islam or even Hinduism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Practices_and_rituals">Practices and rituals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Practices and rituals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masques_BaKongo.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2930" data-file-width="3906" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Masques_BaKongo.JPG/220px-Masques_BaKongo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Masques_BaKongo.JPG/330px-Masques_BaKongo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Masques_BaKongo.JPG/440px-Masques_BaKongo.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masques_BaKongo.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Kongo_people" title="Kongo people">Bakongo</a> masks from the <a href="/wiki/Kongo_Central" title="Kongo Central">Kongo Central</a></div></div></div><p>
There are more similarities than differences in all traditional African religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> The deities and spirits are honored through <a href="/wiki/Libation" title="Libation">libation</a> or sacrifice (of <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">animals</a>, vegetables, cooked food, flowers, semi-precious stones and precious metals). The will of the gods or spirits is sought by the believer also through consultation of divinities or <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Traditional African religions embrace natural phenomena – ebb and tide, waxing and waning moon, rain and drought – and the rhythmic pattern of agriculture. According to Gottlieb and Mbiti: </p><blockquote><p>The environment and nature are infused in every aspect of traditional African religions and culture. This is largely because cosmology and beliefs are intricately intertwined with the natural phenomena and environment. All aspects of weather, thunder, lightning, rain, day, moon, sun, stars, and so on may become amenable to control through the cosmology of African people. Natural phenomena are responsible for providing people with their daily needs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Serer_religion" title="Serer religion">Serer religion</a>, one of the most sacred stars in the cosmos is called Yoonir (<a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">the Star of Sirius</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> With a long farming tradition, the Serer high priests and priestesses (<a href="/wiki/Saltigue" title="Saltigue">Saltigue</a>) deliver yearly sermons at the <a href="/wiki/Xooy" title="Xooy">Xooy</a> Ceremony (divination ceremony) in <a href="/wiki/Fatick" title="Fatick">Fatick</a> before Yoonir's phase in order to predict winter months and enable farmers to start planting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_healers_of_South_Africa" title="Traditional healers of South Africa">Traditional healers</a> are common in most areas, and their practices include a religious element to varying degrees.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Divination">Divination</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Divination">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_20th_century_Yoruba_divination_board.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="743" data-file-width="714" decoding="async" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Early_20th_century_Yoruba_divination_board.jpg/220px-Early_20th_century_Yoruba_divination_board.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Early_20th_century_Yoruba_divination_board.jpg/330px-Early_20th_century_Yoruba_divination_board.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Early_20th_century_Yoruba_divination_board.jpg/440px-Early_20th_century_Yoruba_divination_board.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_20th_century_Yoruba_divination_board.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early-20th-century <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_religion" title="Yoruba religion">Yoruba</a> <a href="/wiki/Divination" title="Divination">divination</a> board</div></div></div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/African_divination" title="African divination">African divination</a></div>
<p>Since Africa is a large continent with many ethnic groups and cultures, there is not one single technique of casting divination. The practice of casting may be done with small objects, such as bones, cowrie shells, stones, strips of leather, or flat pieces of wood.
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sangoma_reading_the_Bones.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Sangoma_reading_the_Bones.jpg/220px-Sangoma_reading_the_Bones.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Sangoma_reading_the_Bones.jpg/330px-Sangoma_reading_the_Bones.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Sangoma_reading_the_Bones.jpg/440px-Sangoma_reading_the_Bones.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sangoma_reading_the_Bones.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_healers_of_South_Africa" title="Traditional healers of South Africa">Traditional healer of South Africa</a> performing a divination by reading the bones</div></div></div>
<p>Some castings are done using sacred divination plates made of wood or performed on the ground (often within a circle).
</p><p>In traditional African societies, many people seek out diviners on a regular basis. There are generally no prohibitions against the practice. Diviner (also known as priest) are also sought for their wisdom as counselors in life and for their knowledge of herbal medicine.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ubuntu">Ubuntu</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Ubuntu">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_philosophy" title="Ubuntu philosophy">Ubuntu philosophy</a></div>
<p>Ubuntu is a <a href="/wiki/Nguni_languages" title="Nguni languages">Nguni</a> <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a> term meaning "humanity". It is sometimes translated as "I am because we are" (also "I am because you are"), or "humanity towards others" (in <a href="/wiki/Zulu_language" title="Zulu language">Zulu</a>, <i>umuntu ngumuntu ngabantu).</i> In <a href="/wiki/Xhosa_language" title="Xhosa language">Xhosa</a>, the latter term is used, but is often meant in a more philosophical sense to mean "the belief in a universal bond of sharing that connects all humanity". It is a collection of values and practices that people of Africa or of African origin view as making people authentic human beings. While the nuances of these values and practices vary across different ethnic groups, they all point to one thing – an authentic individual human being is part of a larger and more significant relational, communal, societal, environmental and spiritual world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Virtue_and_vice">Virtue and vice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Virtue and vice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Virtue in traditional African religion is often connected with carrying out obligations of the communal aspect of life. Examples include social behaviors such as the respect for parents and elders, raising children appropriately, providing hospitality, and being honest, trustworthy, and courageous.
</p><p>In some traditional African religions, morality is associated with obedience or disobedience to God regarding the way a person or a community lives. For the <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people" title="Kikuyu people">Kikuyu</a>, according to their primary supreme <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ngai" title="Ngai">Ngai</a>, acting through the lesser deities, is believed to speak to and be capable of guiding the virtuous person as one's conscience.
</p><p>In many cases, Africans who have converted to other religions have still kept up their traditional customs and practices, combining them in a <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretic</a> way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sacred_places">Sacred places</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sacred places">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some sacred or holy locations for traditional religions include <a href="/wiki/Nri-Igbo" title="Nri-Igbo">Nri-Igbo</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Point_of_Sangomar" title="Point of Sangomar">Point of Sangomar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yaboyabo" title="Yaboyabo">Yaboyabo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fatick" title="Fatick">Fatick</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ife" title="Ife">Ife</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oyo,_Nigeria" title="Oyo, Nigeria">Oyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dahomey" title="Dahomey">Dahomey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benin_City" title="Benin City">Benin City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ouidah" title="Ouidah">Ouidah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nsukka" title="Nsukka">Nsukka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kanem%E2%80%93Bornu_Empire" title="Kanem–Bornu Empire">Kanem-Bornu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Igbo-Ukwu" title="Igbo-Ukwu">Igbo-Ukwu</a>, and Tulwap Kipsigis, among others.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_persecution">Religious persecution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Religious persecution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="excerpt-block"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066933788">.mw-parser-output .excerpt-hat .mw-editsection-like{font-style:normal}</style><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable dablink excerpt-hat selfref" role="note">This section is an excerpt from <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_traditional_African_religion" title="Persecution of traditional African religion">Persecution of traditional African religion</a>.<span class="mw-editsection-like plainlinks"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Persecution_of_traditional_African_religion&amp;action=edit">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div><div class="excerpt">

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar sidebar-collapse nomobile nowraplinks plainlist"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title" style="font-size:160%;font-weight:normal; display:block;"><a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">Freedom of religion</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">
<div class="sidebar-list mw-collapsible mw-collapsed"><div class="sidebar-list-title" style="background:#ddf; text-align:center;">Concepts</div><div class="sidebar-list-content mw-collapsible-content"><div class="hlist hlist-separated">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Religious_discrimination" title="Religious discrimination">Religious discrimination</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Religious_censorship" title="Religious censorship">Religious censorship</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_liberty" title="Religious liberty">Religious liberty</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">Religious pluralism</a></li>

<a href="/wiki/Portal:Religion" title="Portal:Religion">Religion portal</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Religious_freedom" title="Template:Religious freedom"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Religious_freedom" title="Template talk:Religious freedom"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Religious_freedom&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
Traditional African religions have faced <a href="/wiki/Religious_persecution" title="Religious persecution">persecution</a> from Christians and Muslims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Adherents of these religions have been <a href="/wiki/Forced_conversion" title="Forced conversion">forcefully converted</a> to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, demonized and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marginalized" title="Marginalized">marginalized</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The atrocities include killings, waging war, destroying of sacred places, and other atrocities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditions_by_region">Traditions by region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Traditions by region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>This list is limited to a few well-known traditions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Africa">Central Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Central Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bantu_mythology" title="Bantu mythology">Bantu mythology</a> (Central, Southeast, Southern Africa)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bushongo_mythology" title="Bushongo mythology">Bushongo mythology</a> (Congo)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kongo_religion" title="Kongo religion">Kongo religion</a> (Congo)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lugbara_mythology" title="Lugbara mythology">Lugbara mythology</a> (Congo)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baluba_mythology" title="Baluba mythology">Baluba mythology</a> (Congo)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mbuti_mythology" title="Mbuti mythology">Mbuti mythology</a> (Congo)</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hausa_animism" title="Hausa animism">Hausa animism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gabon" title="Gabon">Gabon</a>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotuko_mythology" title="Lotuko mythology">Lotuko mythology</a> (South Sudan)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Eastern_Africa">Eastern Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Eastern Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bantu_mythology" title="Bantu mythology">Bantu mythology</a> (Central, Southeast, Southern Africa)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people#Spirituality_and_religion" title="Kikuyu people">Gikuyu mythology</a> (Kenya)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kamba_people#Culture_and_beliefs" title="Kamba people">Akamba mythology</a> (Kenya)</li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dinka_religion" title="Dinka religion">Dinka religion</a> (South Sudan)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Malagasy_mythology" title="Malagasy mythology">Malagasy mythology</a> (<a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maasai_mythology" title="Maasai mythology">Maasai mythology</a> (Kenya, Tanzania, Ouebian)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kalenjin_mythology" title="Kalenjin mythology">Kalenjin mythology</a> (Kenya, Uganda, Tanzania)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dini_Ya_Msambwa" title="Dini Ya Msambwa">Dini Ya Msambwa</a> (Bungoma, Trans Nzoia, Kenya)</li></ul>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Waaqeffanna" title="Waaqeffanna">Waaqeffanna</a> (Ethiopia)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Somali_mythology" title="Somali mythology">Somali mythology</a> (Somalia)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Northern_Africa">Northern Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Northern Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egyptian_religion" title="Ancient Egyptian religion">Ancient Egyptian religion</a> (Egypt, Sudan)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kemetism" title="Kemetism">Kemetism</a></li></ul></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Punic_religion" title="Punic religion">Punic religion</a> (Tunisia, Algeria, Libya)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Traditional_Berber_religion" title="Traditional Berber religion">Traditional Berber religion</a> (Morocco (including Western Sahara), Algeria, Tunisia, Libya, Egypt, Mauritania, Mali, Niger, Chad, Burkina Faso)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hausa_animism" title="Hausa animism">Hausa animism</a> (<a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Southern_Africa">Southern Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Southern Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bantu_mythology" title="Bantu mythology">Bantu mythology</a> (Central, Southeast, Southern Africa)
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lozi_mythology" title="Lozi mythology">Lozi mythology</a> (Zambia)</li>

<li><a href="/wiki/Badimo" title="Badimo">Badimo</a> (Botswana)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/San_religion" title="San religion">San religion</a> (Botswana, Namibia, and South Africa)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_healers_of_South_Africa" title="Traditional healers of South Africa">Traditional healers of South Africa</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Indigenous_religion_in_Zimbabwe" title="Indigenous religion in Zimbabwe">Indigenous religion in Zimbabwe</a></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Africa">Western Africa</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Western Africa">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ga-Adangme_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ga-Adangme religion (page does not exist)">Ga-Adangme religion</a> (Gana/Ghana)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Abwoi_religion" title="Abwoi religion">Abwoi religion</a> (Nigeria)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Akan_religion" title="Akan religion">Akan religion</a> (Gana/Ghana, Ivory Coast)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dahomean_religion" title="Dahomean religion">Dahomean religion</a> (Benin, Togo)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Efik_religion" title="Efik religion">Efik religion</a> (Nigeria, Cameroon)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edo_religion" title="Edo religion">Edo religion</a> (Benin kingdom, Nigeria)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hausa_animism" title="Hausa animism">Hausa animism</a> (Benin, Burkina Faso, Cameroon, Gana/Ghana, Ivory Coast, Niger, Nigeria, Togo)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ijo_traditional_religion" title="Ijo traditional religion">Ijo traditional religion</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ijo_people" title="Ijo people">Ijo people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Godianism" title="Godianism">Godianism</a> (the umbrella religion of all traditional religions of Africa)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/West_African_mythology" title="West African mythology">West African mythology</a> (the umbrella religion of all traditional religions of West Africa)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Odinala" title="Odinala">Odinala</a> (<a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo people</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asase_Ya" title="Asase Ya">Asaase Yaa</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bono_people" title="Bono people">Bono people</a> (found mostly in Gana/Ghana), Gana/Ghana and Ivory Coast)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Serer_religion" title="Serer religion">Serer religion (A ƭat Roog)</a> (Senegal, Gambia, Mauritania)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yoruba_religion" title="Yoruba religion">Yoruba religion</a> (Nigeria, Benin, Togo)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/West_African_Vodun" title="West African Vodun">Vodou</a> (Gana/Ghana, Benin, Togo, Nigeria)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dogon_religion" title="Dogon religion">Dogon religion</a> (Mali)</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="African_diaspora">African diaspora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Traditional_African_religions&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: African diaspora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/African_diaspora_religions" title="African diaspora religions">African diaspora religions</a></div>
<p>Afro-American religions involve <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancestor_worship" title="Ancestor worship">ancestor worship</a> and include a <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">creator deity</a> along with a <a href="/wiki/Pantheon_(religion)" title="Pantheon (religion)">pantheon</a> of divine spirits such as the <a href="/wiki/Orisha" title="Orisha">Orisha</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loa" title="Loa">Loa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vodun" title="Vodun">Vodun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nkisi" title="Nkisi">Nkisi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alusi" title="Alusi">Alusi</a>, among others. In addition to the <a href="/wiki/Religious_syncretism" title="Religious syncretism">religious syncretism</a> of these various African traditions, many also incorporate elements of <a href="/wiki/Folk_Catholicism" title="Folk Catholicism">Folk Catholicism</a> including <a href="/wiki/Folk_saint" title="Folk saint">folk saints</a> and other forms of <a href="/wiki/Folk_religion" title="Folk religion">Folk religion</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Native_American_religion" title="Native American religion">Native American religion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spiritism" title="Spiritism">Spiritism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spiritualism" title="Spiritualism">Spiritualism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">Shamanism</a> (sometimes including the use of <a href="/wiki/Entheogen" title="Entheogen">Entheogens</a>) and <a href="/wiki/European_folklore" title="European folklore">European folklore</a>.
</p><p>Various "doctoring" spiritual traditions also exist such as <a href="/wiki/Obeah" title="Obeah">Obeah</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoodoo_(folk_magic)" title="Hoodoo (folk magic)">Hoodoo</a> which focus on spiritual health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> African religious traditions in the Americas can vary. They can have non-prominent African roots or can be almost wholly African in nature, such as religions like <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_Orisha" title="Trinidad Orisha">Trinidad Orisha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Encyclbrit-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Encyclbrit_9-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>Britannica Book of the Year</i> (2003), <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica" title="Encyclopædia Britannica">Encyclopædia Britannica</a></i> (2003) <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-85229-956-2" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-85229-956-2">978-0-85229-956-2</a> p.306 <br/>According to the <i>Encyclopædia Britannica</i>, as of mid-2002, there were 480,453,000 Christians, 329,869,000 Muslims and 98,734,000 people who practiced traditional religions in Africa. <a class="external text" href="http://www.bellbookandcandlepublications.com/greenwoodsvillage/gor/islam.php" rel="nofollow">Ian S. Markham, <i>A World Religions Reader</i> (1996) Cambridge, MA: Blackwell Publishers</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160304032618/http://www.bellbookandcandlepublications.com/greenwoodsvillage/gor/islam.php" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> March 4, 2016, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a> is cited by Morehouse University as giving the mid-1990s figure of 278,250,800 Muslims in Africa, but still as 40.8% of the total. These numbers are estimates, and remain a matter of conjecture (see Amadu Jacky Kaba). The spread of Christianity and Islam in Africa: a survey and analysis of the numbers and percentages of Christians, Muslims and those who practice indigenous religions. <i>The Western Journal of Black Studies</i>, Vol 29, Number 2, (June 2005), discusses the estimations of various almanacs and encyclopedias, placing Britannica's estimate as the most agreed on figure. Notes the figure presented at the <a class="external text" href="http://www.afrikaworld.net/afrel/Statistics.htm" rel="nofollow"><i>World Christian Encyclopedia</i>, summarized here</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20160305222924/http://www.afrikaworld.net/afrel/Statistics.htm" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> March 5, 2016, at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, as being an outlier. On rates of growth, Islam and Pentecostal Christianity are highest, see: <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070521093323/http://www.foreignpolicy.com/story/cms.php?story_id=3835" rel="nofollow">The List: The World's Fastest-Growing Religions</a>, Foreign Policy, May 2007.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Lugira-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Lugira_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Lugira, Aloysius M., <i>African Traditional Religions</i> (New York: Chelsea House, 2009), p. 36 [in] Varghese, Roy Abraham, <i>Christ Connection: How the World Religions Prepared the Way for the Phenomenon of Jesus</i>, Paraclete Press (2011), p. 1935, <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781557258397" title="Special:BookSources/9781557258397">9781557258397</a> <a class="external autonumber" href="https://books.google.co.uk/books?id=IH8JtLHnFkoC&amp;pg=PA1935#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">[1]</a> (Retrieved 24 March 2019)</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-googlemix-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-googlemix_11-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMbiti1992">Mbiti, John S (1992). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=4wL0y9fUEB8C&amp;q=%22often+mix%22&amp;pg=PA15" rel="nofollow"><i>Introduction to African religion</i></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780435940027" title="Special:BookSources/9780435940027"><bdi>9780435940027</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Introduction+to+African+religion&amp;rft.date=1992&amp;rft.isbn=9780435940027&amp;rft.aulast=Mbiti&amp;rft.aufirst=John+S&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D4wL0y9fUEB8C%26q%3D%2522often%2Bmix%2522%26pg%3DPA15&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATraditional+African+religions"></span>When Africans are converted to other religions, they often mix their traditional religion with the one to which they are converted. In this way they are not losing something valuable, but are gaining something from both religious customs</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFRiggs2006">Riggs, Thomas (2006). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=uTMOAQAAMAAJ&amp;q=%22many+African+Christians%22" rel="nofollow"><i>Worldmark Encyclopedia of Religious Practices: Religions and denominations</i></a>. p. 1. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780787666125" title="Special:BookSources/9780787666125"><bdi>9780787666125</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Worldmark+Encyclopedia+of+Religious+Practices%3A+Religions+and+denominations&amp;rft.pages=1&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft.isbn=9780787666125&amp;rft.aulast=Riggs&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DuTMOAQAAMAAJ%26q%3D%2522many%2BAfrican%2BChristians%2522&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATraditional+African+religions"></span>Although a large proportion of Africans have converted to Islam an Christianity, these two world religions have been assimilated into African culture, and many African Christians and Muslims maintain traditional spiritual beliefs</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGottlieb2006">Gottlieb, Roger S (2006-11-09). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=_LldeLvqQNsC&amp;pg=PA266" rel="nofollow"><i>The Oxford handbook of religion and ecology</i></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780195178722" title="Special:BookSources/9780195178722"><bdi>9780195178722</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+handbook+of+religion+and+ecology&amp;rft.date=2006-11-09&amp;rft.isbn=9780195178722&amp;rft.aulast=Gottlieb&amp;rft.aufirst=Roger+S&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D_LldeLvqQNsC%26pg%3DPA266&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATraditional+African+religions"></span>Even in the adopted religions of Islam and Christianity, which on the surface appear to have converted millions of Africans from their traditional religions, many aspect of traditional religions are still manifest</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/hostednews/afp/article/ALeqM5hpKESvthJtQeyjk57vyzOnY5l9cg" rel="nofollow">"US study sheds light on Africa's unique religious mix"</a>. AFP.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=US+study+sheds+light+on+Africa%27s+unique+religious+mix&amp;rft.pub=AFP&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.google.com%2Fhostednews%2Fafp%2Farticle%2FALeqM5hpKESvthJtQeyjk57vyzOnY5l9cg&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATraditional+African+religions"></span>t doesn't seem to be an either-or for many people. They can describe themselves primarily as Muslim or Christian and continue to practice many of the traditions that are characteristic of African traditional religion," Luis Lugo, executive director of the Pew Forum, told AFP.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFQuainoo2000">Quainoo, Samuel Ebow (2000-01-01). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=-IDfqT6589kC&amp;pg=PA40" rel="nofollow"><i>In Transitions and consolidation of democracy in Africa</i></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781586840402" title="Special:BookSources/9781586840402"><bdi>9781586840402</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=In+Transitions+and+consolidation+of+democracy+in+Africa&amp;rft.date=2000-01-01&amp;rft.isbn=9781586840402&amp;rft.aulast=Quainoo&amp;rft.aufirst=Samuel+Ebow&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3D-IDfqT6589kC%26pg%3DPA40&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATraditional+African+religions"></span>Even though the two religions are monotheistic, most African Christians and Muslims convert to them and still retain some aspects of their traditional religions</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Encyclopædia Britannica. Britannica Book of the Year 2003. Encyclopædia Britannica, (2003) <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780852299562" title="Special:BookSources/9780852299562">9780852299562</a> p.306. According to the Encyclopædia Britannica, as of mid-2002, there were 376,453,000 Christians, 329,869,000 Muslims and 98,734,000 people who practiced traditional religions in Africa. <a class="external text" href="http://www.greenwoodsvillage.com/gor/islam.htm" rel="nofollow">Ian S. Markham,(A World Religions Reader. Cambridge, Massachusetts: Blackwell Publishers, 1996.)</a> is cited by Morehouse University as giving the mid-1990s figure of 278,250,800 Muslims in Africa, but still as 40.8% of the total. These numbers are estimates, and remain a matter of conjecture. See Amadu Jacky Kaba. The spread of Christianity and Islam in Africa: a survey and analysis of the numbers and percentages of Christians, Muslims and those who practice indigenous religions. The Western Journal of Black Studies, Vol 29, Number 2, June 2005. Discusses the estimations of various almanacs and encyclopedium, placing Britannica's estimate as the most agreed figure. Notes the figure presented at the <a class="external text" href="http://www.afrikaworld.net/afrel/Statistics.htm" rel="nofollow">World Christian Encyclopedia, summarized here</a>, as being an outlier. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_World_Book_Encyclopedia" title="The World Book Encyclopedia">The World Book Encyclopedia</a> has estimated that in 2002 Christians formed 40% of the continent's population, with Muslims forming 45%. It was also estimated in 2002 that Christians form 45% of Africa's population, with Muslims forming 40.6%.</span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/africanvoicesofa00bail" rel="nofollow">Anne C. Bailey, <i>African Voices of the Atlantic Slave Trade: Beyond the Silence and the Shame</i>.</a></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">M. Darrol Bryant, Rita H. Mataragnon, The Many faces of religion and society (1985), Page 100, <a class="external free" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=kv4nAAAAYAAJ" rel="nofollow">https://books.google.com/books?id=kv4nAAAAYAAJ</a>:"African traditional religion went through and survived this type of persecution at the hands of Christianity and Islam..."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-51"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-51">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Garrick Bailey, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?isbn=1133603564" rel="nofollow"><i>Essentials of Cultural Anthropology</i></a>, 3rd edn (2013), p. 268:"Later, during the nineteenth century, Christian missionaries became active in Africa and Oceania. Attempts by Christian missionaries to convert nonbelievers to Christianity took two main forms: forced conversions and proselytizing."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-52"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-52">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">
Festus Ugboaja Ohaegbulam, <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?isbn=0819179418" rel="nofollow"><i>Towards and Understanding of the African Experience</i></a> (1990), p. 161:"The role of Christian missionaries are a private interest group in European colonial occupation of Africa was a significant one...Collectively their activities promoted division within traditional African societies into rival factions...the picture denigrated African culture and religion..."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-53"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-53">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Toyin Falola et al., <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?isbn=031335972" rel="nofollow">Hot Spot: Sub-Saharan Africa: Sub-Saharan Africa</a> (2010), p. 7:"A religion of Middle Eastern origin, Islam reached Africa via the northern region of the continent by means of conquest. The Islamic wars of conquest that would lead to the Islamization of North Africa occurred first in Egypt, when in about 642 CE the country fell to the invading Muslim forces from Arabia. Over the next centuries, the rest of the Maghreb would succumb to Jihadist armies...The notion of religion conversion, whether by force or peaceful means, is foreign to indigenous African beliefs...Islam, however, did not become a religion of the masses by peaceful means. Forced conversion was an indispensable element of proselytization."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFEltisRichardson1997">Eltis, David; Richardson, David (1997). <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=kuXEzQZQmawC&amp;pg=PA88" rel="nofollow"><i>Routes to slavery: direction, ethnicity, and mortality in the transatlantic slave trade</i></a>. <a href="/wiki/Routledge" title="Routledge">Routledge</a>. p. 88. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-7146-4820-5" title="Special:BookSources/0-7146-4820-5"><bdi>0-7146-4820-5</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Routes+to+slavery%3A+direction%2C+ethnicity%2C+and+mortality+in+the+transatlantic+slave+trade&amp;rft.pages=88&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=1997&amp;rft.isbn=0-7146-4820-5&amp;rft.aulast=Eltis&amp;rft.aufirst=David&amp;rft.au=Richardson%2C+David&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DkuXEzQZQmawC%26pg%3DPA88&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATraditional+African+religions"></span></span>
</li>
